template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Scots</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic</a> <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">language</a> <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">variety</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Lowland</a> <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> (where the local dialect is known as <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">Ulster Scots</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> It is sometimes called <b>Lowland Scots</b> to distinguish it from <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic language">Celtic language</a> which was historically restricted to most of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Highlands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galloway" title="Galloway">Galloway</a> after the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The Scots language developed during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> period as a distinct entity.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As there are no universally accepted <a href="/wiki/Language_or_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Language or dialect">criteria for distinguishing</a> a <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a>, scholars and other interested parties often disagree about the linguistic, historical and social status of Scots and particularly its relationship to English.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.894_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.894-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Although a number of paradigms for distinguishing between languages and dialects exist, they often render contradictory results. Broad Scots is at one end of a bipolar linguistic continuum, with <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">Scottish Standard English</a> at the other.<sup id="cite_ref-Stuart-Smith-phonology_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stuart-Smith-phonology-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Scots is often regarded as one of the ancient varieties of English, but it has its own distinct dialects.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.894_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.894-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Alternatively, Scots is sometimes treated as a distinct <a href="/wiki/Germanic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Germanic language">Germanic language</a>, in the way that <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a> is closely linked to but distinct from <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.894_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.894-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2010 <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a> study of "public attitudes towards the Scots language" found that 64% of respondents (around 1,000 individuals being a representative sample of Scotland\'s adult population) "don\'t really think of Scots as a language" but it also found that "the most frequent speakers are least likely to agree that it is not a language (58%) and those never speaking Scots most likely to do so (72%)".<sup id="cite_ref-Public_Attitudes_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Public_Attitudes-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2011#2011_Census_for_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2011">2011 Scottish census</a>, a question on Scots language ability was featured.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Nomenclature"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Nomenclature</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Etymology"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Etymology</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Geographic_distribution"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Geographic distribution</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Status"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Status</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Language_shift"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Language shift</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Language_revitalisation"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Language revitalisation</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Number_of_speakers"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Number of speakers</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Orthography"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Orthography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Sample_text_of_Modern_Scots"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Sample text of Modern Scots</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Grammar"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Grammar</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Phonology"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Phonology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Vowels"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Vowels</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Consonants"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Consonants</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Dictionaries_and_linguistic_information"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Dictionaries and linguistic information</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Collections_of_texts"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Collections of texts</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Native speakers sometimes refer to their <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> as <i>braid Scots</i> (or "broad Scots" in English)<sup id="cite_ref-SND_Scots_adj_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SND_Scots_adj-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> or use a dialect name such as the "<a href="/wiki/Mid_Northern_Scots" class="mw-redirect" title="Mid Northern Scots">Doric</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> or the "Buchan Claik".<sup id="cite_ref-BuchanToulmin1998_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BuchanToulmin1998-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The old-fashioned <i><a href="/wiki/Scotch_(adjective)" title="Scotch (adjective)">Scotch</a></i>, an English loan,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> occurs occasionally, especially in Northern Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>Lallans</i>, a variant of the <a href="/wiki/Modern_Scots" title="Modern Scots">Modern Scots</a> word <i>lawlands</i> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[\xcb\x88lo\xcc\x9c\xcb\x90l\xc9\x99n(d)z, \xcb\x88l\xc9\x91\xcb\x90l\xc9\x99nz]</a></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> is also used, though this is more often taken to mean the <a href="/wiki/Lallans" title="Lallans">Lallans</a> <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary form</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Scots in Ireland is known in official circles as <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">Ulster-Scots</a> (<i>Ulst\xc3\xa8r-Scotch</i> in revivalist Ulster-Scots) or "Ullans", a recent <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">neologism</a> merging Ulster and Lallans.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Scots</i> is a contraction of <i>Scottis</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Early_Scots" title="Early Scots">Older Scots</a><sup id="cite_ref-SND_Scots_adj_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SND_Scots_adj-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and northern version of late <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>Scottisc</i> (modern English "Scottish"), which replaced the earlier <a href="/wiki/Germanic_umlaut" title="Germanic umlaut">i-mutated</a> version <i>Scyttisc</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Before the end of the fifteenth century, English speech in Scotland was known as "English" (written <i>Ynglis</i> or <i>Inglis</i> at the time), whereas "Scottish" (<i>Scottis</i>) referred to <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_(language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaelic (language)">Gaelic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the beginning of the fifteenth century, the English language used in Scotland had arguably become a distinct language, albeit one lacking a name which clearly distinguished it from all the other English variants and dialects spoken in Britain. From 1495 the term <i>Scottis</i> was increasingly used to refer to the Lowland vernacular<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.894_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.894-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and <i>Erse</i>, meaning Irish, as a name for Gaelic. For example, towards the end of the fifteenth century, <a href="/wiki/William_Dunbar" title="William Dunbar">William Dunbar</a> was using <i>Erse</i> to refer to Gaelic and, in the early sixteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Douglas" title="Gavin Douglas">Gavin Douglas</a> was using <i>Scottis</i> as a name for the Lowland vernacular.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The Gaelic of Scotland is now usually called <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Scots_language" title="History of the Scots language">History of the Scots language</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:History_of_Scots_in_Scotland_and_Ulster.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/History_of_Scots_in_Scotland_and_Ulster.png/220px-History_of_Scots_in_Scotland_and_Ulster.png" width="220" height="317" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/History_of_Scots_in_Scotland_and_Ulster.png/330px-History_of_Scots_in_Scotland_and_Ulster.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/History_of_Scots_in_Scotland_and_Ulster.png/440px-History_of_Scots_in_Scotland_and_Ulster.png 2x" data-file-width="2408" data-file-height="3473" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:History_of_Scots_in_Scotland_and_Ulster.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The growth and distribution of Scots in Scotland and Ulster:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> by the beginning of the 9th century in the northern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>, now part of Scotland</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Orange; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Early_Scots" title="Early Scots">Early Scots</a> by the beginning of the 15th century</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Yellow; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Modern_Scots" title="Modern Scots">Modern Scots</a> by the mid 20th century</div></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Northumbrian_(Anglo-Saxon)" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbrian (Anglo-Saxon)">Northumbrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> had been established in what is now southeastern Scotland as far as the <a href="/wiki/River_Forth" title="River Forth">River Forth</a> by the seventh century, as the region was part of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Irish</a> was the then language of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Scottish_monarchs" title="List of Scottish monarchs">Scottish court</a> and the common use of Old English remained largely confined to this area until the thirteenth century. \n</p><p>The succeeding variety of Early northern <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> spoken in southeastern Scotland was also known as <a href="/wiki/Early_Scots" title="Early Scots">Early Scots</a>. It began to further diverge from the Middle English of <a href="/wiki/Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Northumbria">Northumbria</a> due to twelfth and thirteenth century immigration of <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Norse language">Scandinavian-influenced</a> Middle English-speakers from the North and Midlands of England.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Later influences on the development of Scots came from the <a href="/wiki/Romance_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Romance language">Romance languages</a> via ecclesiastical and legal <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman French</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> and later Parisian French, due to the <a href="/wiki/Auld_Alliance" title="Auld Alliance">Auld Alliance</a>. Additionally, there were <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">Middle Low German</a> influences due to trade with and immigration from the <a href="/wiki/Low_Countries" title="Low Countries">Low Countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Scots also includes loan words in the legal and administrative fields resulting from contact with <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Irish</a>, and reflected in early medieval legal documents.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Contemporary <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> loans are mainly for geographical and cultural features, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Ceilidh" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceilidh">ceilidh</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Loch" title="Loch">loch</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Scottish_Clan" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Clan">clan</a></i>.\n</p><p>From the thirteenth century, the Early Scots language spread further into Scotland via the <a href="/wiki/Burgh" title="Burgh">burghs</a>, which were proto-urban institutions first established by King <a href="/wiki/David_I_of_Scotland" title="David I of Scotland">David I</a>. In the fourteenth century Scotland, the growth in prestige of Early Scots and the complementary decline of French, made Scots the <a href="/wiki/Prestige_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestige dialect">prestige dialect</a> of most of eastern Scotland. By the sixteenth century <a href="/wiki/Middle_Scots" title="Middle Scots">Middle Scots</a> had established orthographic and literary norms largely independent of those developing in England.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>From 1610 to the 1690s during the <a href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster" title="Plantation of Ulster">Plantation of Ulster</a> some 200,000 Scots-speaking Lowlanders settled in Ulster in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-Montgomery_&amp;_Gregg_1997:_572_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montgomery_&amp;_Gregg_1997:_572-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In the core areas of Scots settlement, Scots outnumbered English settlers by five or six to one.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>The name <a href="/wiki/Modern_Scots" title="Modern Scots">Modern Scots</a> is used to describe the Scots language after 1700, when southern <a href="/wiki/Modern_English" title="Modern English">Modern English</a> was generally adopted as the literary language, though Modern Scots remained the vernacular.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In Scotland, Scots is spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Lowlands" title="Scottish Lowlands">Scottish Lowlands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Isles" title="Northern Isles">Northern Isles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">Caithness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Arran" title="Isle of Arran">Arran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Campbeltown" title="Campbeltown">Campbeltown</a>. In Ulster (Ireland) it is spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Counties_of_Ireland" title="Counties of Ireland">Counties</a> of <a href="/wiki/County_Down" title="County Down">Down</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Antrim" title="County Antrim">Antrim</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Londonderry" title="County Londonderry">Londonderry</a> and <a href="/wiki/County_Donegal" title="County Donegal">Donegal</a>. Dialects include <a href="/wiki/Insular_Scots" title="Insular Scots">Insular Scots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Scots" title="Northern Scots">Northern Scots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Scots" title="Central Scots">Central Scots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_Scots" title="Southern Scots">Southern Scots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_dialects" title="Ulster Scots dialects">Ulster Scots</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lufe_God_Abufe_Al_-_John_Knox_House_200411.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Lufe_God_Abufe_Al_-_John_Knox_House_200411.jpg/220px-Lufe_God_Abufe_Al_-_John_Knox_House_200411.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Lufe_God_Abufe_Al_-_John_Knox_House_200411.jpg/330px-Lufe_God_Abufe_Al_-_John_Knox_House_200411.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Lufe_God_Abufe_Al_-_John_Knox_House_200411.jpg/440px-Lufe_God_Abufe_Al_-_John_Knox_House_200411.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1760" data-file-height="1168" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lufe_God_Abufe_Al_-_John_Knox_House_200411.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><i lang="sco" title="Scots language text">Lufe God abufe al and yi nychtbour as yi self</i></i> ("Love God above all and thy neighbour as thyself"), an example of <a href="/wiki/Early_Scots" title="Early Scots">Early Scots</a> on <a href="/wiki/John_Knox_House" title="John Knox House">John Knox House</a>, Edinburgh</div></div></div>\n<p>Before the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Union_1707" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Union 1707">Treaty of Union 1707</a>, when Scotland and England joined to form the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>, there is ample evidence that Scots was widely held to be an independent <a href="/wiki/Sister_language" title="Sister language">sister language</a><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> forming a <a href="/wiki/Pluricentric_language" title="Pluricentric language">pluricentric</a> <a href="/wiki/Diasystem" title="Diasystem">diasystem</a> with English.\n</p><p>The linguist <a href="/wiki/Heinz_Kloss" title="Heinz Kloss">Heinz Kloss</a> considered Modern Scots a <i>Halbsprache</i> (half language) in terms of an <a href="/wiki/Abstand_and_ausbau_languages" title="Abstand and ausbau languages">abstand and ausbau languages</a> framework<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> although today, in Scotland, most people\'s speech is somewhere on a continuum ranging from traditional broad Scots to <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">Scottish Standard English</a>. Many speakers are either <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossic</a> and/or able to <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">code-switch</a> along the continuum depending on the situation in which they find themselves. Where on this continuum English-influenced Scots becomes Scots-influenced English is difficult to determine. Because standard English now generally has the role of a <a href="/wiki/Dachsprache" class="mw-redirect" title="Dachsprache">Dachsprache</a>, disputes often arise as to whether the varieties of Scots are dialects of Scottish English or constitute a separate language in their own right.<sup id="cite_ref-Stuart-Smith_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stuart-Smith-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maggie_Mcott_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maggie_Mcott-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The UK government now accepts Scots as a <a href="/wiki/Regional_language" title="Regional language">regional language</a> and has recognised it as such under the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Notwithstanding the UK government\'s and the Scottish Executive\'s obligations under part II of the European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages, the Scottish Executive recognises and respects Scots (in all its forms) as a distinct language, and does not consider the use of Scots to be an indication of poor competence in English.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Evidence for its existence as a separate language lies in the extensive body of Scots literature, its independent \xe2\x80\x93 if somewhat fluid \xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthographic conventions</a> and in its former use as the language of the original <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Because Scotland retained distinct political, legal, and religious systems after the Union, many Scots terms passed into Scottish English.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_shift">Language shift</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Language shift">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From the mid-sixteenth century, written Scots was increasingly influenced by the developing <a href="/wiki/Standard_English" title="Standard English">Standard English</a> of Southern England due to developments in royal and political interactions with England.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When an <a href="/wiki/William_Flower_(officer_of_arms)" title="William Flower (officer of arms)">English herald</a> spoke to <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Guise" title="Mary of Guise">Mary of Guise</a> and her councillors in 1560, at first they spoke in the "Scottyshe toung", but then he "not well understanding", they continued in her native French.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">King James VI</a>, who in 1603 became <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I of England</a>, observed in his work <i>The Reulis and Cautelis to be observit and eschewit in Scottis Prose</i> that "For albeit sindrie has written of it in Engish, quhilk is lykest to our language ..." however, with the increasing influence and availability of books printed in England, most writing in Scotland came to be done in the English fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-Scots&#39;_2003_p._11_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scots&#39;_2003_p._11-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Following James VI\'s move to London, the Protestant Church of Scotland adopted the 1611 <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">Authorized King James Version</a> of the Bible; subsequently, the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a> led to England joining Scotland to form the Kingdom of Great Britain, having a single Parliament of Great Britain based in London. After the Union and the shift of political power to England, the use of Scots was discouraged by many in authority and education, as was the notion of Scottishness itself.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Many leading Scots of the period, such as <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, defined themselves as <a href="/wiki/North_Britain" title="North Britain">Northern British</a> rather than Scottish.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> They attempted to rid themselves of their Scots in a bid to establish standard English as the official language of the newly formed union. Nevertheless, Scots was still spoken across a wide range of domains until the end of the eighteenth century,<sup id="cite_ref-Scots&#39;_2003_p._11_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scots&#39;_2003_p._11-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> illustrated for example, in the summary by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Frederick_Albert_Pottle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Frederick Albert Pottle (page does not exist)">Frederick Pottle</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Boswell" title="James Boswell">James Boswell</a>\'s twentieth-century biographer, concerning James\'s view of the speech habits of his father <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Boswell_(judge)" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Boswell (judge)">Alexander Boswell</a>, a judge of the <a href="/wiki/College_of_Justice" title="College of Justice">Supreme Courts</a> of Scotland:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>He scorned modern literature, spoke broad Scots from the bench, and even in writing took no pains to avoid the Scotticisms which most of his colleagues were coming to regard as vulgar.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Others did however scorn Scots, such as intellectuals from the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Enlightenment" title="Scottish Enlightenment">Scottish Enlightenment</a> David Hume and <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>, who went to great lengths to get rid of every Scotticism from their writings.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Following such examples, many well-off Scots took to learning English through the activities of those such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sheridan_(actor)" title="Thomas Sheridan (actor)">Thomas Sheridan</a>, who in 1761 gave a series of lectures on English <a href="/wiki/Elocution" title="Elocution">elocution</a>. Charging a <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(British_coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinea (British coin)">guinea</a> at a time (about \xc2\xa3100 in today\'s money<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>), they were attended by over 300 men, and he was made a <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/freeman" class="extiw" title="wikt:freeman">freeman</a> of the City of <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>. Following this, some of the city\'s intellectuals formed the Select Society for Promoting the Reading and Speaking of the English Language in Scotland. From such eighteenth-century activities grew <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">Scottish Standard English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Scots remained the vernacular of many rural communities and the growing number of urban working-class Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AU_Burns_Canberra.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/AU_Burns_Canberra.jpg/220px-AU_Burns_Canberra.jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/AU_Burns_Canberra.jpg/330px-AU_Burns_Canberra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/AU_Burns_Canberra.jpg/440px-AU_Burns_Canberra.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="578" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AU_Burns_Canberra.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canberra,_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Canberra, Australia">Canberra, Australia</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries, the use of Scots as a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a> was revived by several prominent Scotsmen such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a>. Such writers established a new cross-dialect literary norm.\n</p><p>During the first half of the twentieth century, knowledge of eighteenth- and nineteenth-century literary norms waned, and currently there is no institutionalised standard literary form.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> By the 1940s, the Scottish Education Department\'s <a href="/wiki/Language_policy" title="Language policy">language policy</a> was that Scots had no value: "it is not the language of \'educated\' people anywhere, and could not be described as a suitable medium of education or culture".<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Students reverted to Scots outside the classroom, but the reversion was not complete. What occurred, and has been occurring ever since, is a process of <a href="/wiki/Language_attrition" title="Language attrition">language attrition</a>, whereby successive generations have adopted more and more features from Standard English. This process has accelerated rapidly since widespread access to mass media in English and increased population mobility became available after the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> It has recently taken on the nature of wholesale <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">language shift</a>, sometimes also termed language <a href="/wiki/Language_change" title="Language change">change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Language_convergence" title="Language convergence">convergence</a> or <a href="/wiki/Language_merger" class="mw-redirect" title="Language merger">merger</a>. By the end of the twentieth century, Scots was at an advanced stage of <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">language death</a> over much of Lowland Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Residual features of Scots are often regarded as slang.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a> study in 2010 found that 64% of respondents (being a representative sample of Scotland\'s adult population) "don\'t really think of Scots as a language", however, "the most frequent speakers are least likely to agree that it is not a language (58%) and those never speaking Scots most likely to do so (72%)".<sup id="cite_ref-Public_Attitudes_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Public_Attitudes-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_revitalisation">Language revitalisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Language revitalisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Wye_Smith-The_New_Testament_in_Braid_Scots.pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/William_Wye_Smith-The_New_Testament_in_Braid_Scots.pdf/page1-220px-William_Wye_Smith-The_New_Testament_in_Braid_Scots.pdf.jpg" width="220" height="352" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/William_Wye_Smith-The_New_Testament_in_Braid_Scots.pdf/page1-330px-William_Wye_Smith-The_New_Testament_in_Braid_Scots.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/William_Wye_Smith-The_New_Testament_in_Braid_Scots.pdf/page1-440px-William_Wye_Smith-The_New_Testament_in_Braid_Scots.pdf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="798" data-file-height="1277" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Wye_Smith-The_New_Testament_in_Braid_Scots.pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>William Wye Smith\'s <i>The <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> in Braid Scots</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Recently, attitudes have somewhat changed, and the status of the language has been raised in schools in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Scots is now included in the new national school <a href="/wiki/Curriculum_for_Excellence" title="Curriculum for Excellence">curriculum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Previously in Scotland\'s schools there had been little education taking place through the <a href="/wiki/Medium_of_instruction" title="Medium of instruction">medium</a> of Scots, although it may have been covered superficially in English lessons, which could entail reading some Scots literature and observing the local dialect. Much of the material used was often Standard English disguised as Scots, which caused upset among proponents of Standard English and proponents of Scots alike.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> One example of the educational establishment\'s approach to Scots is, "Write a poem in Scots. (It is important not to be worried about spelling in this \xe2\x80\x93 write as you hear the sounds in your head.)",<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> whereas guidelines for English require teaching pupils to be "writing fluently and legibly with accurate spelling and punctuation".<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The use of Scots in the media is scant and is usually reserved for niches where local dialect is deemed acceptable, e.g. comedy, <a href="/wiki/Burns_supper" title="Burns supper">Burns Night</a>, or representations of traditions and times gone by. Serious use for news, encyclopaedias, documentaries, etc., rarely occurs in Scots, although the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a> website has offered some information in it.<sup id="cite_ref-test_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-test-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_speakers">Number of speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Number of speakers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/300px-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" width="300" height="445" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/450px-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/600px-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 2x" data-file-width="9970" data-file-height="14790" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The proportion of respondents in the 2011 census in Scotland aged 3 and above who stated that they can speak Scots.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulster-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ulster-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png/300px-Ulster-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png" width="300" height="234" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ulster-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png/450px-Ulster-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Ulster-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png/600px-Ulster-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png 2x" data-file-width="20838" data-file-height="16226" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ulster-Scots_speakers_in_the_2011_census_in_Northern_Ireland.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The proportion of respondents in the 2011 census in Northern Ireland aged 3 and above who stated that they can speak Ulster-Scots.</div></div></div>\n<p>It has been difficult to determine the number of speakers of Scots via census, because many respondents might interpret the question "Do you speak Scots?" in different ways. Campaigners for Scots pressed for this question to be included in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">2001 UK National Census</a>. The results from a 1996 trial before the Census, by the <a href="/wiki/General_Register_Office_for_Scotland" title="General Register Office for Scotland">General Register Office for Scotland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Mate_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mate-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> suggested that there were around 1.5 million speakers of Scots, with 30% of Scots responding "Yes" to the question "Can you speak the Scots language?", but only 17% responding "Aye" to the question "Can you speak Scots?".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> (It was also found that older, working-class people were more likely to answer in the affirmative.) The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">University of Aberdeen</a> Scots Leid Quorum performed its own research in 1995, cautiously suggesting that there were 2.7&#160;million speakers, though with clarification as to why these figures required context.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The GRO questions, as freely acknowledged by those who set them, were not as detailed and as systematic as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">University of Aberdeen</a> ones, and only included reared speakers, not those who had learned the language. Part of the difference resulted from the central question posed by surveys: "Do you speak Scots?". In the Aberdeen University study, the question was augmented with the further clause "...&#160;or a dialect of Scots such as Border etc.", which resulted in greater recognition from respondents. The GRO concluded that there simply was not enough linguistic self-awareness amongst the Scottish populace, with people still thinking of themselves as speaking badly pronounced, grammatically inferior English rather than Scots, for an accurate census to be taken. The GRO research concluded that "[a] more precise estimate of genuine Scots language ability would require a more in-depth interview survey and may involve asking various questions about the language used in different situations. Such an approach would be inappropriate for a Census." Thus, although it was acknowledged that the "inclusion of such a Census question would undoubtedly raise the profile of Scots", no question about Scots was, in the end, included in the 2001 Census.<sup id="cite_ref-Stuart-Smith_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stuart-Smith-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish Government\'s <i>Pupils in Scotland Census 2008</i><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> found that 306 pupils spoke Scots as their main home language. A <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a> study in 2010 found that 85% of around 1000 respondents (being a representative sample of Scotland\'s adult population) claim to speak Scots to varying degrees.<sup id="cite_ref-Public_Attitudes_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Public_Attitudes-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2011 UK census was the first to ask residents of Scotland about Scots. A campaign called Aye Can was set up to help individuals answer the question.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The specific wording used was "Which of these can you do? Tick all that apply" with options for \'Understand\', \'Speak\', \'Read\' and \'Write\' in three columns: English, Scottish Gaelic and Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Of approximately 5.1&#160;million respondents, about 1.2&#160;million (24%) could speak, read and write Scots, 3.2&#160;million (62%) had no skills in Scots and the remainder had some degree of skill, such as understanding Scots (0.27&#160;million, 5.2%) or being able to speak it but not read or write it (0.18&#160;million, 3.5%).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> There were also small numbers of Scots speakers recorded in England and Wales on the 2011 Census, with the largest numbers being either in bordering areas (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Carlisle,_Cumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Carlisle, Cumbria">Carlisle</a>) or in areas that had recruited large numbers of Scottish workers in the past (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Corby" title="Corby">Corby</a> or the former mining areas of <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scots-language_literature" title="Scots-language literature">Scots-language literature</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:854px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm/854px--WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm.jpg&quot; controls=&quot;&quot; preload=&quot;none&quot; autoplay=&quot;&quot; style=&quot;width:854px;height:480px&quot; class=&quot;kskin&quot; data-durationhint=&quot;553.171&quot; data-startoffset=&quot;0&quot; data-mwtitle=&quot;WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm&quot; data-mwprovider=&quot;wikimediacommons&quot;&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4e/WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm.480p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth WebM (160P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 160P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;160p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;284&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Small WebM (240P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 240P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;240p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;426&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;WebM (360P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 360P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;360p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;640&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;568880&quot; data-framerate=&quot;59.94&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:WIKITONGUES- Christine speaking Shetlandic.webm" style="width:220px;height:124px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm/220px--WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4e/WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WIKITONGUES-_Christine_speaking_Shetlandic.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wikitongues" title="Wikitongues">Wikitongues</a> - Scottish poet <a href="/wiki/Christine_De_Luca" title="Christine De Luca">Christine De Luca</a> speaking the Shetlandic variety of Scots</div></div></div>\n<p>Among the earliest Scots literature is <a href="/wiki/John_Barbour_(poet)" title="John Barbour (poet)">John Barbour\'s</a> <i>Brus</i> (fourteenth century), <a href="/wiki/Wyntoun" class="mw-redirect" title="Wyntoun">Wyntoun</a>\'s <i>Cronykil</i> and <a href="/wiki/Blind_Harry" title="Blind Harry">Blind Harry</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Actes_and_Deidis_of_the_Illustre_and_Vallyeant_Campioun_Schir_William_Wallace" class="mw-redirect" title="The Actes and Deidis of the Illustre and Vallyeant Campioun Schir William Wallace">The Wallace</a></i> (fifteenth century). From the fifteenth century, much literature based on the Royal Court in Edinburgh and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_St_Andrews" title="University of St Andrews">University of St Andrews</a> was produced by writers such as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Henryson" title="Robert Henryson">Robert Henryson</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Dunbar" title="William Dunbar">William Dunbar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Douglas" title="Gavin Douglas">Gavin Douglas</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Lyndsay" title="David Lyndsay">David Lyndsay</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Complaynt_of_Scotland" title="The Complaynt of Scotland">The Complaynt of Scotland</a></i> was an early printed work in Scots. The <i><a href="/wiki/Eneados" title="Eneados">Eneados</a></i> is a <a href="/wiki/Middle_Scots" title="Middle Scots">Middle Scots</a> translation of <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Aeneid" title="Aeneid">Aeneid</a></i>, completed by Gavin Douglas in 1513.\n</p><p>After the seventeenth century, anglicisation increased. At the time, many of the oral ballads from the <a href="/wiki/Border_ballads" class="mw-redirect" title="Border ballads">borders</a> and the North East were written down. Writers of the period were <a href="/wiki/Robert_Sempill" title="Robert Sempill">Robert Sempill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Sempill_the_younger" title="Robert Sempill the younger">Robert Sempill the younger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Sempill" title="Francis Sempill">Francis Sempill</a>, Lady Wardlaw and Lady <a href="/wiki/Grizel_Baillie" class="mw-redirect" title="Grizel Baillie">Grizel Baillie</a>.\n</p><p>In the eighteenth century, writers such as <a href="/wiki/Allan_Ramsay_(poet)" title="Allan Ramsay (poet)">Allan Ramsay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns" title="Robert Burns">Robert Burns</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Orr_(poet)" title="James Orr (poet)">James Orr</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Fergusson" title="Robert Fergusson">Robert Fergusson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Scott" title="Walter Scott">Walter Scott</a> continued to use Scots \xe2\x80\x93 Burns\'s "<a href="/wiki/Auld_Lang_Syne" title="Auld Lang Syne">Auld Lang Syne</a>" is in Scots, for example. Scott introduced vernacular dialogue to his novels. Other well-known authors like <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>, William Alexander, <a href="/wiki/George_MacDonald" title="George MacDonald">George MacDonald</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._M._Barrie" title="J. M. Barrie">J. M. Barrie</a> and other members of the <a href="/wiki/Kailyard_school" title="Kailyard school">Kailyard school</a> like <a href="/wiki/Ian_Maclaren" title="Ian Maclaren">Ian Maclaren</a> also wrote in Scots or used it in dialogue.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a> popular Scottish newspapers regularly included articles and commentary in the vernacular, often of unprecedented proportions.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early twentieth century, a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Renaissance" title="Scottish Renaissance">renaissance</a> in the use of Scots occurred, its most vocal figure being <a href="/wiki/Hugh_MacDiarmid" title="Hugh MacDiarmid">Hugh MacDiarmid</a> whose benchmark poem "<a href="/wiki/A_Drunk_Man_Looks_at_the_Thistle" title="A Drunk Man Looks at the Thistle">A Drunk Man Looks at the Thistle</a>" (1926) did much to demonstrate the power of Scots as a modern idiom. Other contemporaries were <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Young_(classicist)" title="Douglas Young (classicist)">Douglas Young</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Buchan,_1st_Baron_Tweedsmuir" class="mw-redirect" title="John Buchan, 1st Baron Tweedsmuir">John Buchan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Goodsir_Smith" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidney Goodsir Smith">Sidney Goodsir Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Garioch" title="Robert Garioch">Robert Garioch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edith_Anne_Robertson" title="Edith Anne Robertson">Edith Anne Robertson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_McLellan" title="Robert McLellan">Robert McLellan</a>. The revival extended to verse and other literature.\n</p><p>In 1955 three <a href="/wiki/Ayrshire" title="Ayrshire">Ayrshire</a> men \xe2\x80\x93 Sandy MacMillan, an English teacher at <a href="/wiki/Ayr_Academy" title="Ayr Academy">Ayr Academy</a>; Thomas Limond, noted town Chamberlain of <a href="/wiki/Ayr" title="Ayr">Ayr</a>; and A.L. (Ross) Taylor, Rector of Cumnock Academy &#160;&#8211;&#32; collaborated to write <i>Bairnsangs</i> (Child Songs),<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> a collection of children\'s <a href="/wiki/Nursery_rhyme" title="Nursery rhyme">nursery rhymes</a> and poems in Scots. The book contains a five-page glossary of contemporary Scots words and their pronunciations.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Gray_(poet)" title="Alexander Gray (poet)">Alexander Gray</a>\'s translations into Scots constitute the greater part of his work, and are the main basis for his reputation.\n</p><p>In 1983 <a href="/wiki/William_Laughton_Lorimer" class="mw-redirect" title="William Laughton Lorimer">William Laughton Lorimer</a>\'s translation of the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> from the original Greek was published.\n</p><p>Highly anglicised Scots is sometimes used in contemporary fiction, for example, the Edinburgh dialect of Scots in <i><a href="/wiki/Trainspotting_(novel)" title="Trainspotting (novel)">Trainspotting</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Irvine_Welsh" title="Irvine Welsh">Irvine Welsh</a> (later made into a <a href="/wiki/Trainspotting_(film)" title="Trainspotting (film)">motion picture of the same name</a>).\n</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/But%27n%27Ben_A-Go-Go" title="But&#39;n&#39;Ben A-Go-Go">But\'n\'Ben A-Go-Go</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Fitt" title="Matthew Fitt">Matthew Fitt</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Cyberpunk" title="Cyberpunk">cyberpunk</a> novel written entirely in what Wir Ain Leid<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> (Our Own Language) calls "General Scots". Like all cyberpunk work, it contains imaginative <a href="/wiki/Neologism" title="Neologism">neologisms</a>.\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Rubaiyat_of_Omar_Khayyam" title="Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam">Rubaiyat of Omar Khayyam</a></i> has been translated into Scots by <a href="/wiki/Rab_Wilson" title="Rab Wilson">Rab Wilson</a> (published in 2004). Alexander Hutchison has translated the poetry of <a href="/wiki/Catullus" title="Catullus">Catullus</a> into Scots, and in the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Liz_Lochhead" title="Liz Lochhead">Liz Lochhead</a> produced a Scots translation of <i><a href="/wiki/Tartuffe" title="Tartuffe">Tartuffe</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Moli\xc3\xa8re">Moli\xc3\xa8re</a>. <a href="/wiki/J._K._Annand" title="J. K. Annand">J. K. Annand</a> translated poetry and fiction from German and medieval Latin into Scots.\n</p><p>The strip cartoons <i><a href="/wiki/Oor_Wullie" title="Oor Wullie">Oor Wullie</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Broons" title="The Broons">The Broons</a></i> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Sunday_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Sunday Post">Sunday Post</a></i> use some Scots.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Early_Scots" title="Early Scots">Early Scots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Scots" title="Middle Scots">Middle Scots</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Scots" title="Modern Scots">Modern Scots</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> of <a href="/wiki/Early_Scots" title="Early Scots">Early Scots</a> had become more or less standardised<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> by the middle to late sixteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Union_of_the_Crowns" title="Union of the Crowns">Union of the Crowns</a> in 1603 the <a href="/wiki/Standard_English" title="Standard English">Standard English</a> of England came to have an increasing influence on the spelling of Scots<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> through the increasing influence and availability of books printed in England. After the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union</a> in 1707 the emerging <a href="/wiki/Scottish_English" title="Scottish English">Scottish form of Standard English</a> replaced Scots for most formal writing in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-Scots&#39;_2003_p._11_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scots&#39;_2003_p._11-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The eighteenth-century Scots revival saw the introduction of a new <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a> descended from the old court Scots, but with an orthography that had abandoned some of the more distinctive old Scots spellings<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and adopted many standard English spellings. Despite the updated spelling, however, the rhymes make it clear that a Scots pronunciation was intended.<sup id="cite_ref-David_D._Murison_1976_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_D._Murison_1976-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> These writings also introduced what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Apologetic_apostrophe" title="Apologetic apostrophe">apologetic apostrophe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> generally occurring where a <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonant</a> exists in the Standard English <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a>. This Written Scots drew not only on the vernacular but also on the <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">King James Bible</a> and was also heavily influenced by the norms and conventions of <a href="/wiki/Augustan_literature" title="Augustan literature">Augustan English poetry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, this written Scots looked very similar to contemporary Standard English, suggesting a somewhat modified version of that, rather than a distinct speech form with a phonological system which had been developing independently for many centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> This modern literary dialect, \xe2\x80\x98Scots of the book\' or Standard Scots<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> once again gave Scots an orthography of its own, lacking neither "authority nor author."<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> This literary language used throughout Lowland Scotland and Ulster,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> embodied by writers such as Allan Ramsay, Robert Fergusson, Robert Burns, Sir Walter Scott, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Murray_(poet)" title="Charles Murray (poet)">Charles Murray</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Herbison&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="David Herbison (page does not exist)">David Herbison</a>, James Orr, <a href="/wiki/James_Hogg" title="James Hogg">James Hogg</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Laidlaw" title="William Laidlaw">William Laidlaw</a> among others, is well described in the 1921 Manual of Modern Scots.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other authors developed dialect writing, preferring to represent their own speech in a more phonological manner rather than following the pan-dialect conventions of modern literary Scots,<sup id="cite_ref-David_D._Murison_1976_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-David_D._Murison_1976-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> especially for the northern<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> and insular dialects of Scots.\n</p><p>During the twentieth century a number of proposals for spelling reform were presented. Commenting on this, John Corbett (2003: 260) writes that "devising a normative orthography for Scots has been one of the greatest linguistic hobbies of the past century." Most proposals entailed regularising the use of established eighteenth and nineteenth century conventions, in particular the avoidance of the <a href="/wiki/Apologetic_apostrophe" title="Apologetic apostrophe">apologetic apostrophe</a> which supposedly represented "missing" English letters. Such letters were never actually missing in Scots. For example, in the fourteenth century, <a href="/wiki/John_Barbour_(poet)" title="John Barbour (poet)">Barbour</a> spelt the Scots <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> of \'taken\' as <i>tane</i>. Because there has been no <i>k</i> in the word for over 700 years, representing its omission with an apostrophe seems pointless. The current spelling is usually <i>taen</i>.\n</p><p>Through the twentieth century, with the decline of spoken Scots and knowledge of the literary tradition, phonetic (often humorous) representations became more common.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_text_of_Modern_Scots">Sample text of Modern Scots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Sample text of Modern Scots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>From <i>The New Testament in Scots</i> (William Laughton Lorimer 1885\xe2\x80\x931967)\nMatthew:1:18ff\n</p>\n<dl><dd>This is the storie o the birth o Jesus Christ. His mither Mary wis trystit til Joseph, but afore they war mairriet she wis fund tae be wi bairn bi the Halie Sp\xc3\xadrit. Her husband Joseph, honest man, hed nae mind tae affront her afore the warld an wis for brakkin aff their tryst hidlinweys; an sae he wis een ettlin tae dae, whan an angel o the Lord kythed til him in a draim an said til him, "Joseph, son o Dauvit, be nane feared tae tak Mary your trystit wife intil your hame; the bairn she is cairrein is o the Halie Sp\xc3\xadrit. She will beir a son, an the name ye ar tae g\xc3\xade him is Jesus, for he will sauf his fowk frae their sins."</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>Aa this happent at the wurd spokken bi the Lord throu the Prophet micht be fulfilled: Behaud, the virgin wil bouk an beir a son, an they will caa his name Immanuel \xe2\x80\x93 that is, "God wi us".</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd>Whan he hed waukit frae his sleep, Joseph did as the angel hed bidden him, an tuik his trystit wife hame wi him. But he bedditna wi her or she buir a son; an he caa\xe2\x80\x99d the bairn Jesus.</dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Scots#Grammar" title="Modern Scots">Modern Scots grammar</a></div>\n<p>Modern Scots follows the <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" title="Subject\xe2\x80\x93verb\xe2\x80\x93object">subject\xe2\x80\x93verb\xe2\x80\x93object</a> sentence structure as does <a href="/wiki/Standard_English" title="Standard English">Standard English</a>. However, the word order <i>He turnt oot the licht</i> to \'He turned the light out\' and <i>Gie\'s it</i> (<i>Give us it</i>) to \'Give it to me\' may be preferred.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.897_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.897-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The indefinite <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">article</a> <i>a</i> may be used before both consonants and vowels. The definite article <i>the</i> is used before the names of seasons, days of the week, many nouns, diseases, trades and occupations, sciences and academic subjects.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> It is also often used in place of the indefinite article and instead of a <a href="/wiki/Possessive_pronoun" class="mw-redirect" title="Possessive pronoun">possessive pronoun</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scots includes some strong <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plurals</a> such as <i>ee</i>/<i>een</i> (eye/eyes), <i>cauf</i>/<i>caur</i> (calf/calves), <i>horse</i>/<i>horse</i> (horse/horses), <i>cou</i>/<i>kye</i> (cow/cows) and <i>shae</i>/<i>shuin</i> (shoe/shoes) that survived from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> into Modern Scots but have become weak plurals in Standard Modern English \xe2\x80\x93 <i>ox</i>/<i>oxen</i> and <i>child</i>/<i>children</i> being exceptions.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.896_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.896-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">Nouns</a> of measure and quantity remain unchanged in the plural.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.896_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.896-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Relative_pronoun" title="Relative pronoun">relative pronoun</a> is <i>that</i> for all persons and numbers, but may be elided.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.896_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.896-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Modern Scots also has a third adjective/adverb <i>this</i>-<i>that</i>-<i>yon</i>/<i>yonder</i> (<i>thon</i>/<i>thonder</i>) indicating something at some distance.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.896_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.896-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> <i>Thir</i> and <i>thae</i> are the plurals of <i>this</i> and <i>that</i> respectively.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present tense</a> of <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verbs</a> adheres to the <a href="/wiki/Northern_subject_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern subject rule">Northern subject rule</a> whereby verbs end in -<i>s</i> in all persons and numbers except when a single personal pronoun is next to the verb.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.896_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.896-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Certain verbs are often used progressively<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.896_94-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.896-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> and verbs of motion may be dropped before an adverb or adverbial phrase of motion.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.897_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.897-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many verbs have <a href="/wiki/Germanic_strong_verb" title="Germanic strong verb">strong</a> or <a href="/wiki/Irregular_verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Irregular verb">irregular</a> forms which are distinctive from Standard English.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.896_94-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.896-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\nThe regular past form of the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_weak_verb" title="Germanic weak verb">weak</a> or <a href="/wiki/Regular_verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Regular verb">regular</a> verbs is <i>-it</i>, <i>-t</i> or <i>-ed</i>, according to the preceding consonant or vowel.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Language_1992._p.896_94-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Language_1992._p.896-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Present_participle" class="mw-redirect" title="Present participle">present participle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">gerund</a> <i>in</i> are now usually <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99n/</span><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> but may still be differentiated <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99n/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/in/</span> in Southern Scots<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x99n/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\xaan/</span> North Northern Scots.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Negation_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Negation (linguistics)">negative particle</a> is <i>na</i>, sometimes spelled <i>nae</i>, e.g. <i>canna</i> (can\'t), <i>daurna</i> (daren\'t), <i>michtna</i> (mightn\'t).<sup id="cite_ref-Grant,_William_1921_p.115_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant,_William_1921_p.115-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Adverb" title="Adverb">Adverbs</a> usually take the same form as the verb root or <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjective</a> especially after verbs. <i>Haein a real guid day</i> (Having a really good day). <i>She\'s awfu fauchelt</i> (She\'s awfully tired).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Modern_Scots#Phonology" title="Modern Scots">Modern Scots \xc2\xa7&#160;Phonology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phonological_history_of_Scots" title="Phonological history of Scots">Phonological history of Scots</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scots_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section:
